class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Mandiant Israel Research Team</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2>Background</h2>
<p>Over the last year Mandiant has been tracking UNC3890, a cluster of activity targeting Israeli shipping, government, energy and healthcare organizations via social engineering lures and a potential watering hole. Mandiant assesses with moderate confidence this actor is linked to Iran, which is notable given the strong focus on shipping and the ongoing naval conflict between Iran and Israel. While we believe this actor is focused on intelligence collection, the collected data may be leveraged to support various activities, from hack-and-leak, to enabling kinetic warfare attacks like those that have plagued the shipping industry in recent years.</p>
<p>Mandiant assesses with moderate confidence that UNC3890 conducts espionage and intelligence collection activity to support multiple Iranian interests and operations. Targeting patterns indicate a strong interest in Israeli entities and organizations of various sectors, including government, shipping, energy and healthcare. We observed several limited technical connections to Iran, such as PDB strings and Farsi language artifacts.</p>
<p>This campaign has been active since at least late 2020, and is still ongoing as of mid-2022, and though it is regional in nature, targeted entities include global companies.</p>
<p>UNC3890 uses at least two unique tools: a backdoor which we named SUGARUSH, and a browser credential stealer, which exfiltrates stolen data via Gmail, Yahoo and Yandex email services that we’ve named SUGARDUMP. UNC3890 also uses multiple publicly available tools, such as the METASPLOIT framework and NorthStar C2.</p>
<p>In addition, Mandiant discovered UNC3890 operates an inter-connected network of Command-and-Control (C2) servers. The C2 servers host domains and fake login pages spoofing legitimate services such as Office 365, social networks such as LinkedIn and Facebook, as well as fake job offers and fake commercials for AI-based robotic dolls. We observed the C2 servers communicating with multiple targets, as well as with a watering hole that we believe was targeting the Israeli shipping sector, in particular entities that handle and ship sensitive components.</p>
<p>This blog post details the activity of UNC3890, including their proprietary malware, TTPs we have not previously seen deployed by Iran, and the publicly available tools we identified in our investigation. Mandiant continues to track UNC3890 as well as other potentially related clusters of activity by the same threat actor.</p>
<h3><strong>Attribution</strong></h3>
<p>Mandiant uses the label “UNC” groups – or “uncategorized” groups – to refer to a cluster of intrusion activity that includes observable artifacts, such as adversary infrastructure , tools, and tradecraft that we are not yet ready to give a classification such as TEMP, APT, or FIN (learn more about <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/how-mandiant-tracks-uncategorized-threat-actors" rel="noopener" target="_blank">how Mandiant tracks uncategorized threat actors</a>). Mandiant found no significant connections between UNC3890 and other clusters of activities we currently track, and therefore sees it as a standalone group. However, we identified several connections suggesting the activity is conducted by an Iran-nexus group:</p>
<ul>
<li>Usage of Farsi words, as observed in strings left by the developers in the newest version of SUGARDUMP, for example “KHODA” (the Farsi word for “God”) and “yaal” (the Farsi word for a horse’s mane).</li>
<li>Focused targeting of Israeli entities and organizations, or organizations operating in Israel, consistent with other clusters of activity operated by Iranian threat actors, specifically UNC757.</li>
<li>Usage of the same PDB path as another Iranian cluster of activity Mandiant tracks as UNC2448 (operated by the Iranian IRGC, according to public sources), publicly referred to in a U.S. government <a href="https://www.cisa.gov/uscert/ncas/alerts/aa21-321a" rel="noopener" target="_blank">statement</a> from November 17, 2021. Several publications suggested that UNC2448 is <a href="https://thedfirreport.com/2021/11/15/exchange-exploit-leads-to-domain-wide-ransomware/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">linked to APT35/Charming Kitten</a> cluster of activities, which according to several public sources is <a href="https://attack.mitre.org/groups/G0059/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">operated by the Iranian Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC)</a>. UNC2448 has been targeting Israeli entities as well, among other countries of interest to Iran.</li>
<li>Utilization of NorthStar C2 Framework, a C2 framework preferred by other Iranian actors . However, since it is a publicly available framework used by multiple threat actors, we consider this link circumstantial.</li>
</ul>
<h3><strong>Targeting</strong></h3>
<p>In late 2021, Mandiant identified UNC3890 targeting Israeli entities and showing interest in various sectors, including government, shipping, energy, aviation and healthcare. Even though the targeting we observed is focused to Israel, some of the entities targeted by UNC3890, especially in the shipping sector, are global companies. Therefore, the potential impact of UNC3890 activity described in this blog may extend beyond Israel. The activity is consistent with historical Iranian interest in these targets. Targeting patterns and lures used by UNC3890 indicate an attempt to disguise their activity as legitimate login activity, legitimate services and social network applications, and technology-related visual content.</p>
<h3><strong>Malware Observed</strong></h3>
<p>Mandiant observed UNC3890 deploy the following malware families.</p>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%"><table>

<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px"><strong>Malware Family</strong></td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px"><strong>Description</strong></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">SUGARUSH</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">SUGARUSH is a backdoor written to establish a connection with an embedded C2 and to execute CMD commands.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">SUGARDUMP</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">SUGARDUMP is a credential harvesting utility, capable of password collection from Chromium-based browsers.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">SUGARDUMP SMTP-based</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">A more advanced version of SUGARDUMP, exfiltrating the stolen credentials via Gmail, Yahoo and Yandex email addresses. Uses a commercial for robotic dolls as a lure.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">SUGARDUMP HTTP-based</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">The newest version of SUGARDUMP, exfiltrating the stolen credentials to a dedicated server over HTTP. Uses a fake job offer as a lure.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">METASPLOIT</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">METASPLOIT is a penetration testing software, often abused by malicious threat actors.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">UNICORN</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">UNICORN is a publicly available tool for conducting a PowerShell downgrade attack and to inject a shellcode into memory.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">NORTHSTAR C2</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">NORTHSTAR C2 is an open-source C2 framework developed for penetration testing and red teaming.</td>
</tr>

<h3><strong>Outlook and Implications</strong></h3>
<p>UNC3890 has been operating since at least late 2020. Their focused targeting poses a threat to Israel-based organizations and entities, particularly those affiliated with the government, shipping, energy, aviation and healthcare sectors. While we are not aware of targeting outside Israel, it is possible such targeting has occurred, or will occur. UNC3890 utilization of legitimate or publicly available tools, in addition to their unique exfiltration method using Gmail, Yahoo and Yandex email addresses, may reflect their efforts to evade detection and to bypass heuristics or network-based security measures.</p>
<h3><strong>UNC3890 Attack Lifecycle</strong></h3>
<h4>Establish Foothold</h4>
<p>While Mandiant primarily identified post-exploitation implants utilized by UNC3890, there are some findings that shed light about their initial access methodologies. Mandiant identified UNC3890 potentially used the following initial access vectors:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Watering holes</strong> – Mandiant identified a potential watering hole hosted on a login page of a legitimate Israeli shipping company, which was likely compromised by UNC3890. The watering hole was active at least until November 2021, and upon entering the legitimate login page, the user would be sending a POST request with preliminary data about the logged user to an attacker controlled non-ASCII <span lang="EN-IE">Punycode </span>domain (lirıkedin[.]com, interpreted as xn--lirkedin-vkb[.]com).</li>
</ul>
<p>The URL structure of the POST request:</p>
<p><em>hxxps[:]//xn--lirkedin-vkb[.]com/object[.]php?browser=&lt;user_browser&gt;&amp;ip=&lt;user_ip&gt;</em></p>
<p>When we inspected the watering hole, it was already inactive, but it was most likely used to target clients and users of that Israeli shipping company<span lang="EN-IE">, in particular, one’s shipping or handling heat-sensitive cargo (based on the nature of the compromised website)</span>. We have an additional indication of an attempted targeting of another major Israeli shipping company by UNC3890, which is consistent with the watering hole.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Credentials harvesting by masquerading as legitimate services</strong> – we uncovered several domains resolving to UNC3890’s C2 servers. Some of the domains were masquerading as legitimate services and entities, as can be observed in the table below. UNC3890 may have used these domains to harvest credentials to legitimate services, to send phishing lures, or to overall mask their activity and blend in with expected network traffic.</li>
</ul>
<p>It should be noted that many of these domains were hosted on the same infrastructure used by UNC3890, but date back to late 2020, which is before we can corroborate UNC3890 has been active.</p>
<p> </p>

<p>In addition, we identified an UNC3890 server that hosted several ZIP files containing scraped contents of Facebook and Instagram accounts of legitimate individuals. It is possible they were targeted by UNC3890, or used as lures in a social engineering effort.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush1_0_lhoz.max-700x700.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush1_0_lhoz.max-700x700.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush1_0_lhoz.max-700x700.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush1_0_lhoz.max-700x700.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 1: A fake Outlook Web Access login page hosted on UNC3890’s domain naturaldolls[.]store</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><strong>Fake job offers</strong>, potentially as part of a phishing or watering hole campaign – we observed UNC3890 utilize a .xls lure file designed as a fake job offer (MD5: 639f83fa4265ddbb43e85b763fe3dbac) which installs SUGARDUMP, a credential harvesting tool. The job offer was for a software developer position in LexisNexis, a company offering a data analytics solution.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush2_jqqz.max-1400x1400.jpg" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush2_jqqz.max-1400x1400.jpg" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush2_jqqz.max-1400x1400.jpg" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush2_jqqz.max-1400x1400.jpg" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 2: A fake LexisNexis job offer which drops SUGARDUMP</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><ul>
<li><strong>Fake commercials for AI-based robotic dolls</strong> – one of UNC3890’s most recent endeavor to target victims includes the usage of a video commercial for AI-based robotic dolls, used as a lure to deliver SUGARDUMP. In addition, we observed UNC3890 usage of domains with similar themes such as naturaldolls[.]store (hosting a fake Outlook login page) and xxx-doll[.]com. In addition, UNC3890 infrastructure hosted a fake page for the alleged purchasing of robotic dolls, redirecting victims to an attacker controlled infrastructure.</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h4>Post-Exploitation: From Credentials Harvesting to Full Access and Control</h4>
<p>After gaining initial access, UNC3890 utilizes a broad toolset to access and control the victim’s environment. In this sector we will focus on the analysis of two of UNC3890’s proprietary tools we identified in our investigation: the SUGARUSH backdoor, and the SUGARDUMP credential harvesting tool. We will also provide a brief analysis of the public tools utilized by UNC3890.</p>
<p>Most of the tools were available for download directly from UNC3890 controlled infrastructure, indicating they may have been served as 1<sup>st</sup> stage implants. Alternatively, they may have been used as 2<sup>nd</sup> stages (or later), but UNC3890 may have misconfigured their infrastructure, making it publicly accessible.</p>
<p><u>SUGARUSH Analysis: A Small But Efficient Backdoor</u></p>
<p>SUGARUSH is a small custom backdoor that establishes a reverse shell over TCP to a hardcoded C&amp;C address.</p>
<p>Upon first execution, SUGARUSH will create a new service called “Service1”. Subsequently, SUGARUSH creates a logging folder called “Logs”, and stores it under its current execution path. A new folder named<br>“ServiceLog” is created in the “Logs” folder, and a new log file is written with the message “Service is started at ”. The name of the log files is the current date of the infected machine.</p>
<p>SUGARUSH will then check for internet connectivity of the host and will create a log file indicating the result with message “You are online at ” or “You are offline at ”. If the attempt for internet connection is successful, SUGARUSH will establish a new TCP connection to an embedded C&amp;C address via port 4585.</p>
<p>SUGARUSH then waits to receive an answer from the C2 which will be interpreted as a CMD command for execution.</p>
<p>SUGARUSH Samples:</p>
<ul>
<li>37bdb9ea33b2fe621587c887f6fb2989</li>
<li>3f045ebb014d859a4e7d15a4cf827957</li>
<li>a7a2d6a533b913bc50d14e91bcf6c716</li>
<li>d528e96271e791fab5818c01d4bc139f</li>
</ul>
<p><u>SUGARDUMP Analysis: A Browser Credential Harvesting Tool</u></p>
<p>SUGARDUMP is a small custom utility used for harvesting credentials from Chrome, Opera and Edge Chromium browsers.</p>
<p>When executed SUGARDUMP will access the following paths:</p>
<ul>
<li>%AppData%\\Google\\Chrome\\User Data</li>
<li>%AppData&amp;\\Opera Software\\Opera Stable</li>
<li>%AppData%\\Microsoft\\Edge\\User Data</li>
</ul>
<p>Out of each path SUGARDUMP will attempt collect specific folders:</p>
<ul>
<li>\\Default\\Login Data</li>
<li>\\Login Data</li>
<li>Any other folder that has the string “Profile” in its name.</li>
</ul>
<p>Afterwards, SUGARDUMP will extract all of the available usernames and passwords from these folders.</p>
<p>The collected information is subsequently stored in the following format:</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush5.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush5.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush5.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush5.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 5: SUGARDUMP exfiltrated data format</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>We observed several versions of SUGARDUMP:</p>
<ul>
<li>SUGARDUMP first known version, dated to early 2021. This early version stores the credentials without exfiltrating them. It is possible it was an unfinished version, or that UNC3890 used other tools and/or manually connect to the victim to exfiltrate the stolen credentials.</li>
<li>SUGARDUMP using SMTP-based communication, dated to late 2021-early 2022. This version utilizes Yahoo, Yandex and Gmail addresses for exfiltration, and uses a commercial AI-driven robotic dolls as a lure.</li>
<li>SUGARDUMP using HTTPs-based communication, dated to April 2022. This version uses a fake NexisLexis job offer as a lure.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>SUGARDUMP first known version</strong> – dated to early 2021, we observed two variants of this version: the first one saves the stolen credentials under in a .txt file under the path: “C:\\Users\\User\\Desktop\\test2.txt”. The second variant prints the stolen credentials as a CMD output.</p>
<p>We observed two PDB paths contained in SUGARDUMP samples:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>C:\Users\User\source\repos</strong>\passrecover\passrecover\obj\Release\passrecover.pdb – we observed a similar PDB path (the part in <strong>bold</strong>) used in a toolset (for example, MD5: 69b2ab3369823032991d4b306a170425) by UNC2448, an actor affiliated with Iran, which was mentioned in a U.S. government statement in November 17, 2021. Since this is a rather generic PDB path, this similarity may be circumstantial, and we consider it a weak link.</li>
<li>C:\Users\User\Desktop\sourc\Chrome-Password-Recovery-master\Chrome-Password-Recovery-master\obj\Debug\ChromeRecovery.pdb</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>SUGARDUMP using SMTP for C2 communication</strong> – dated to late 2021-early 2022. This variant was downloaded from a known UNC3890 C2 (URL: hxxp://128.199.6[.]246/3-Video-VLC.exe), and is a slightly more advanced version with similar credential harvesting functionality.</p>
<p>The downloaded file “3-Video-VLC.exe” (MD5: ae0a16b6feddd53d1d52ff50d85a42d5) is a Windows installer which, upon execution, drops and executes two files under the path %AppData%<span lang="EN-IE">\\Roaming\\</span>:</p>
<ol>
<li>CrashReporter.exe (MD5: 084ad50044d6650f9ed314e99351a608) – a browser credential harvesting tool (SUGARDUMP).</li>
<li>RealDo1080.mp4 (MD5: d8fb3b6f5681cf5eec2b89be9b632b05) – a social engineering video, played using Windows Media Player while CrashReporter.exe is executed. The video contains a commercial for AI-driven robotic dolls.</li>
</ol>
<p>Upon first execution, CrashReporter.exe (SUGARDUMP) attempts to locate the folder: <em>%AppData%\\Microsoft\\Edge\\User Data\\CrashPad\\</em></p>
<p>If it wasn’t found it will search for folder: <em>%AppData%\\Microsoft\\Internet Explorer\\TabRoaming\\</em></p>
<p>If the latter folder is not found as well, the malware proceeds to create it. The malware will then copy itself into “TabRoaming” folder again under the name “CrashReporter.exe”. Subsequently, a scheduled task is created, which ensures the persistence of this version of SUGARDUMP:</p>
<ul>
<li>In Windows 7 the scheduled task is called: "MicrosoftInternetExplorerCrashRepoeterTaskMachineUA", and contains the description <em>"Keep your Microsoft software without any bugs. If this task is disabled or stopped, your Microsoft software may not work properly, meaning bugs that may arise cannot be fixed and features may not work.”</em></li>
<li>In other Windows OS versions the scheduled task is called: "MicrosoftEdgeCrashRepoeterTaskMachineUA", and contains the description <em>“Keep your Microsoft software without any bugs. If this task is disabled or stopped, your Edge browser may not work properly, meaning bugs that may arise cannot be fixed and features may not work.”</em></li>
</ul>
<p>The scheduled task is configured to execute CrashReporter.exe during user logon.</p>
<p>The malware then attempts to connect to “smtp.yandex.com” and “smtp.mail.yahoo.com” via port 587. If the attempt is successful, the malware starts to harvest bowser related information on the host.</p>
<p>This version of SUGARDUMP harvest credentials from the following browsers:</p>
<ul>
<li>Firefox (added functionality with relation to <span lang="EN-IE">the previous version</span>)</li>
<li>Chrome</li>
<li>Opera</li>
<li>Edge</li>
</ul>
<p>For each browser the malware attempts to extract login credentials from the following paths:</p>
<ul>
<li>%Appdata%\\Mozilla\\Firefox\\Profiles</li>
<li>%Appdata%\\Google\\Chrome\\User Data</li>
<li>%Appdata%\\Opera Software\\Opera Stable</li>
<li>%Appdata%\\Microsoft\\Edge\\User Data</li>
</ul>
<p>This version of SUGARDUMP also extracts the browser’s version, browsing history, bookmarks, and cookies.</p>
<p>The extracted data structure looks as follows:</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush6_0_yfay.max-1300x1300.jpg" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush6_0_yfay.max-1300x1300.jpg" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush6_0_yfay.max-1300x1300.jpg" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/SugarRush6_0_yfay.max-1300x1300.jpg" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 6: Exfiltrated data format of SUGARDUMP</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>The collected data is subsequently encoded using base64 and stored under: <em>%%\\CrashLog.txt</em></p>
<p>The malware will then send the file “CrashLog.txt” via email, by connecting and sending it from one of the two following email addresses:</p>
<ul>
<li>john.macperson2021@yandex[.]com</li>
<li>john.macperson2021@yahoo[.]com</li>
</ul>
<p>The email is sent to one of these four email addresses:</p>
<ul>
<li>john.macperson2021@yandex[.]com</li>
<li>john.macperson2021@yahoo[.]com</li>
<li>john.macperson2021@gmail[.]com</li>
<li>john.macperson@protonmail[.]com</li>
</ul>
<p>The subject for each message would be “VLC Player”, with “CrashLog.txt” attached.</p>
<p>If SUGARDUMP fails to send the message, it creates a new file under: <em>%%\\CrashLogName.txt</em>, and writes to the file the error details. "CrashLogName.txt" is also sent via email, using the same method mentioned above. Afterwards, the malware terminates its execution.</p>
<p><strong>SUGARDUMP using HTTP for C2 communication </strong>– dated to April 2022, this version sends the stolen credentials to an UNC3890 C2 server (144.202.123[.]248:80). We observed this version dropped by a .xls file which contains a fake job offer to a software developer position in NexisLexis, a data analytics platform (MD5: 639f83fa4265ddbb43e85b763fe3dbac).</p>
<p>The .xls file contains a Macro, which upon enablement attempts to execute an embedded PE file using RunDLL (MD5: e125ed072fc4529687d98cf4c62e283e). The PE file is the newest version of SUGARDUMP we observed so far.</p>
<p>Like previous versions, this version of SUGARDUMP harvests credentials from Chromium-based browsers Chrome, Opera and Edge. The data is saved in a new file under <em>%TEMP%\\DebugLogWindowsDefender.txt</em>.</p>
<p>The collected data is subsequently encrypted using AES encryption using Cipher Block Chaining (CBC) mode. The encryption key is the Sha256 of an embedded password: “1qazXSW@3edc123456be name KHODA 110 !!)1qazXSW@3edc”. The word “KHODA” means god in Farsi.</p>
<p>After the encryption process, the data is also encoded using Base64, and subsequently sent over HTTP to an UNC3890 C2 server: 144.202.123[.]248:80.</p>
<p>The .NET project for this version of SUGARDUMP was named "yaal", which is the Farsi word for a horse’s mane. This, along with the use of the word “KHODA” in SUGARDUMP’s encryption key, may strengthen the possibility that the developers of SUGARDUMP are Farsi speakers.</p>
<p>SUGARDUMP Samples:</p>
<ul>
<li>f362a2d9194a09eaca7d2fa04d89e1e5 – early version</li>
<li>08dc5c2af21ecee6f2b25ebdd02a9079 – early version</li>
<li>ae0a16b6feddd53d1d52ff50d85a42d5 – SMTP-based version</li>
<li>e125ed072fc4529687d98cf4c62e283e – HTTP-based version</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>MITRE ATT&amp;CK Techniques</strong></p>
<p>Resource Development<br><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1588/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><strong>Obtain Capabilities</strong></a> (T1588)</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1588/002/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><strong>Tool</strong></a> (T1588.002)</li>
</ul>

Sectors"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Mandiant Israel Research Team\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003eBackground\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOver the last year Mandiant has been tracking UNC3890, a cluster of activity targeting Israeli shipping, government, energy and healthcare organizations via social engineering lures and a potential watering hole. Mandiant assesses with moderate confidence this actor is linked to Iran, which is notable given the strong focus on shipping and the ongoing naval conflict between Iran and Israel. While we believe this actor is focused on intelligence collection, the collected data may be leveraged to support various activities, from hack-and-leak, to enabling kinetic warfare attacks like those that have plagued the shipping industry in recent years.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant assesses with moderate confidence that UNC3890 conducts espionage and intelligence collection activity to support multiple Iranian interests and operations. Targeting patterns indicate a strong interest in Israeli entities and organizations of various sectors, including government, shipping, energy and healthcare. We observed several limited technical connections to Iran, such as PDB strings and Farsi language artifacts.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis campaign has been active since at least late 2020, and is still ongoing as of mid-2022, and though it is regional in nature, targeted entities include global companies.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUNC3890 uses at least two unique tools: a backdoor which we named SUGARUSH, and a browser credential stealer, which exfiltrates stolen data via Gmail, Yahoo and Yandex email services that we’ve named SUGARDUMP. UNC3890 also uses multiple publicly available tools, such as the METASPLOIT framework and NorthStar C2.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn addition, Mandiant discovered UNC3890 operates an inter-connected network of Command-and-Control (C2) servers. The C2 servers host domains and fake login pages spoofing legitimate services such as Office 365, social networks such as LinkedIn and Facebook, as well as fake job offers and fake commercials for AI-based robotic dolls. We observed the C2 servers communicating with multiple targets, as well as with a watering hole that we believe was targeting the Israeli shipping sector, in particular entities that handle and ship sensitive components.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis blog post details the activity of UNC3890, including their proprietary malware, TTPs we have not previously seen deployed by Iran, and the publicly available tools we identified in our investigation. Mandiant continues to track UNC3890 as well as other potentially related clusters of activity by the same threat actor.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAttribution\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant uses the label “UNC” groups – or “uncategorized” groups – to refer to a cluster of intrusion activity that includes observable artifacts, such as adversary infrastructure , tools, and tradecraft that we are not yet ready to give a classification such as TEMP, APT, or FIN (learn more about \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/how-mandiant-tracks-uncategorized-threat-actors\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003ehow Mandiant tracks uncategorized threat actors\u003c/a\u003e). Mandiant found no significant connections between UNC3890 and other clusters of activities we currently track, and therefore sees it as a standalone group. However, we identified several connections suggesting the activity is conducted by an Iran-nexus group:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUsage of Farsi words, as observed in strings left by the developers in the newest version of SUGARDUMP, for example “KHODA” (the Farsi word for “God”) and “yaal” (the Farsi word for a horse’s mane).\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eFocused targeting of Israeli entities and organizations, or organizations operating in Israel, consistent with other clusters of activity operated by Iranian threat actors, specifically UNC757.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUsage of the same PDB path as another Iranian cluster of activity Mandiant tracks as UNC2448 (operated by the Iranian IRGC, according to public sources), publicly referred to in a U.S. government \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.cisa.gov/uscert/ncas/alerts/aa21-321a\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003estatement\u003c/a\u003e from November 17, 2021. Several publications suggested that UNC2448 is \u003ca href\u003d\"https://thedfirreport.com/2021/11/15/exchange-exploit-leads-to-domain-wide-ransomware/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003elinked to APT35/Charming Kitten\u003c/a\u003e cluster of activities, which according to several public sources is \u003ca href\u003d\"https://attack.mitre.org/groups/G0059/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eoperated by the Iranian Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps (IRGC)\u003c/a\u003e. UNC2448 has been targeting Israeli entities as well, among other countries of interest to Iran.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUtilization of NorthStar C2 Framework, a C2 framework preferred by other Iranian actors . However, since it is a publicly available framework used by multiple threat actors, we consider this link circumstantial.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eTargeting\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn late 2021, Mandiant identified UNC3890 targeting Israeli entities and showing interest in various sectors, including government, shipping, energy, aviation and healthcare. Even though the targeting we observed is focused to Israel, some of the entities targeted by UNC3890, especially in the shipping sector, are global companies. Therefore, the potential impact of UNC3890 activity described in this blog may extend beyond Israel. The activity is consistent with historical Iranian interest in these targets. Targeting patterns and lures used by UNC3890 indicate an attempt to disguise their activity as legitimate login activity, legitimate services and social network applications, and technology-related visual content.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMalware Observed\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant observed UNC3890 deploy the following malware families.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMalware Family\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDescription\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eSUGARUSH\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eSUGARUSH is a backdoor written to establish a connection with an embedded C2 and to execute CMD commands.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eSUGARDUMP\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eSUGARDUMP is a credential harvesting utility, capable of password collection from Chromium-based browsers.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eSUGARDUMP SMTP-based\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eA more advanced version of SUGARDUMP, exfiltrating the stolen credentials via Gmail, Yahoo and Yandex email addresses. Uses a commercial for robotic dolls as a lure.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eSUGARDUMP HTTP-based\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eThe newest version of SUGARDUMP, exfiltrating the stolen credentials to a dedicated server over HTTP. Uses a fake job offer as a lure.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eMETASPLOIT\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eMETASPLOIT is a penetration testing software, often abused by malicious threat actors.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eUNICORN\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eUNICORN is a publicly available tool for conducting a PowerShell downgrade attack and to inject a shellcode into memory.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eNORTHSTAR C2\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eNORTHSTAR C2 is an open-source C2 framework developed for penetration testing and red teaming.\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eOutlook and Implications\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUNC3890 has been operating since at least late 2020. Their focused targeting poses a threat to Israel-based organizations and entities, particularly those affiliated with the government, shipping, energy, aviation and healthcare sectors. While we are not aware of targeting outside Israel, it is possible such targeting has occurred, or will occur. UNC3890 utilization of legitimate or publicly available tools, in addition to their unique exfiltration method using Gmail, Yahoo and Yandex email addresses, may reflect their efforts to evade detection and to bypass heuristics or network-based security measures.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUNC3890 Attack Lifecycle\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003eEstablish Foothold\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile Mandiant primarily identified post-exploitation implants utilized by UNC3890, there are some findings that shed light about their initial access methodologies. Mandiant identified UNC3890 potentially used the following initial access vectors:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWatering holes\u003c/strong\u003e – Mandiant identified a potential watering hole hosted on a login page of a legitimate Israeli shipping company, which was likely compromised by UNC3890. The watering hole was active at least until November 2021, and upon entering the legitimate login page, the user would be sending a POST request with preliminary data about the logged user to an attacker controlled non-ASCII \u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003ePunycode \u003c/span\u003edomain (lirıkedin[.]com, interpreted as xn--lirkedin-vkb[.]com).\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe URL structure of the POST request:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003ehxxps[:]//xn--lirkedin-vkb[.]com/object[.]php?browser\u003d\u0026lt;user_browser\u0026gt;\u0026amp;ip\u003d\u0026lt;user_ip\u0026gt;\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhen we inspected the watering hole, it was already inactive, but it was most likely used to target clients and users of that Israeli shipping company\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e, in particular, one’s shipping or handling heat-sensitive cargo (based on the nature of the compromised website)\u003c/span\u003e. We have an additional indication of an attempted targeting of another major Israeli shipping company by UNC3890, which is consistent with the watering hole.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eCredentials harvesting by masquerading as legitimate services\u003c/strong\u003e – we uncovered several domains resolving to UNC3890’s C2 servers. Some of the domains were masquerading as legitimate services and entities, as can be observed in the table below. UNC3890 may have used these domains to harvest credentials to legitimate services, to send phishing lures, or to overall mask their activity and blend in with expected network traffic.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt should be noted that many of these domains were hosted on the same infrastructure used by UNC3890, but date back to late 2020, which is before we can corroborate UNC3890 has been active.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv addition, we identified an UNC3890 server that hosted several ZIP files containing scraped contents of Facebook and Instagram accounts of legitimate individuals. In this sector we will focus on the analysis of two of UNC3890’s proprietary tools we identified in our investigation: the SUGARUSH backdoor, and the SUGARDUMP credential harvesting tool. We will also provide a brief analysis of the public tools utilized by UNC3890.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMost of the tools were available for download directly from UNC3890 controlled infrastructure, indicating they may have been served as 1\u003csup\u003est\u003c/sup\u003e stage implants. Alternatively, they may have been used as 2\u003csup\u003end\u003c/sup\u003e stages (or later), but UNC3890 may have misconfigured their infrastructure, making it publicly accessible.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cu\u003eSUGARUSH Analysis: A Small But Efficient Backdoor\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSUGARUSH is a small custom backdoor that establishes a reverse shell over TCP to a hardcoded C\u0026amp;C address.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUpon first execution, SUGARUSH will create a new service called “Service1”. Subsequently, SUGARUSH creates a logging folder called “Logs”, and stores it under its current execution path. A new folder named\u003cbr\u003e“ServiceLog” is created in the “Logs” folder, and a new log file is written with the message “Service is started at ”. The name of the log files is the current date of the infected machine.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSUGARUSH will then check for internet connectivity of the host and will create a log file indicating the result with message “You are online at ” or “You are offline at ”. If the attempt for internet connection is successful, SUGARUSH will establish a new TCP connection to an embedded C\u0026amp;C address via port 4585.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSUGARUSH then waits to receive an answer from the C2 which will be interpreted as a CMD command for execution.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSUGARUSH Samples:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e37bdb9ea33b2fe621587c887f6fb2989\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e3f045ebb014d859a4e7d15a4cf827957\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ea7a2d6a533b913bc50d14e91bcf6c716\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ed528e96271e791fab5818c01d4bc139f\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cu\u003eSUGARDUMP Analysis: A Browser Credential Harvesting Tool\u003c/u\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSUGARDUMP is a small custom utility used for harvesting credentials from Chrome, Opera and Edge Chromium browsers.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhen executed SUGARDUMP will access the following paths:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e%AppData%\\\\Google\\\\Chrome\\\\User Data\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e%AppData\u0026amp;\\\\Opera Software\\\\Opera Stable\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e%AppData%\\\\Microsoft\\\\Edge\\\\User Data\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOut of each path SUGARDUMP will attempt collect specific folders:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\\\\Default\\\\Login Data\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\\\\Login Data\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAny other folder that has the string “Profile” in its name.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAfterwards, SUGARDUMP will extract all of the available usernames and passwords from these folders.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe collected information is subsequently stored in the following format:\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 5: SUGARDUMP exfiltrated data SUGARDUMP:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eSUGARDUMP first known version, dated to early 2021. Since this is a rather generic PDB path, this similarity may be circumstantial, and we consider it a weak link.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eC:\\Users\\User\\Desktop\\sourc\\Chrome-Password-Recovery-master\\Chrome-Password-Recovery-master\\obj\\Debug\\ChromeRecovery.pdb\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSUGARDUMP using SMTP for C2 communication\u003c/strong\u003e – dated to late 2021-early 2022. The video contains a commercial for AI-driven robotic dolls.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUpon first execution, CrashReporter.exe (SUGARDUMP) attempts to locate the folder: \u003cem\u003e%AppData%\\\\Microsoft\\\\Edge\\\\User Data\\\\CrashPad\\\\\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIf it wasn’t found it will search for folder: \u003cem\u003e%AppData%\\\\Microsoft\\\\Internet Explorer\\\\TabRoaming\\\\\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIf the latter folder is not found as well, the malware proceeds to create it. The malware will then copy itself into “TabRoaming” folder again under the name “CrashReporter.exe”. Subsequently, a scheduled task is created, which ensures the persistence of this version of SUGARDUMP:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIn Windows 7 the scheduled task is called: \"MicrosoftInternetExplorerCrashRepoeterTaskMachineUA\", and contains the description \u003cem\u003e\"Keep your Microsoft software without any bugs. If this task is disabled or stopped, your Microsoft software may not work properly, meaning bugs that may arise cannot be fixed and features may not work.”\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIn other Windows OS versions the scheduled task is called: \"MicrosoftEdgeCrashRepoeterTaskMachineUA\", and contains the description \u003cem\u003e“Keep your Microsoft software without any bugs. If this task is disabled or stopped, your Edge browser may not work properly, meaning bugs that may arise cannot be fixed and features may not work.”\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe scheduled task is configured to execute CrashReporter.exe during user logon.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe malware then attempts to connect to “smtp.yandex.com” and “smtp.mail.yahoo.com” via port 587. If the attempt is successful, the malware starts to harvest bowser related information on the host.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis version of SUGARDUMP harvest credentials from the following browsers:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eFirefox (added functionality with relation to \u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003ethe previous The data is saved in a new file under \u003cem\u003e%TEMP%\\\\DebugLogWindowsDefender.txt\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe collected data is subsequently encrypted using AES encryption using Cipher Block Chaining (CBC) mode. The encryption key is the Sha256 of an embedded password: “1qazXSW@3edc123456be name KHODA 110 !!)1qazXSW@3edc”. The word “KHODA” means god in Farsi.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAfter the encryption process, the data is also encoded using Base64, and subsequently sent over HTTP to an UNC3890 C2 server: 144.202.123[.]248:80.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe .NET project for this version of SUGARDUMP was named \"yaal\", which is the Farsi word for a horse’s mane.
